The fix replaces `CompletableFuture.supplyAsync` + `IntStream` + `join()` with the standard `ExecutorService.submit()` + `Future.get()` in `for` loops — a proven pattern with no stream/lambda/future interaction edge cases. Also splits segmentation and OCR into separate executors with different concurrency limits.

```
161:     private void runPipeline(File inputFile, File outputFile,
162:                              ImageSegmenter segmenter,
163:                              OCREngine ocrEngine, JBIG2Compressor compressor,
164:                              PDFAssembler assembler, boolean useMrc, boolean usePdfa,

167:         // Create temp directory for this run's intermediate files

169:         File tempDir = new File("temp/" + inputName + "-" + System.nanoTime());
170:         tempDir.mkdirs();
171: 
172:         try (PDDocument source = Loader.loadPDF(inputFile)) {
173:             PDFRenderer renderer = new PDFRenderer(source);
174:             int pageCount = source.getNumberOfPages();
175:             long totalStart = System.nanoTime();
176: 
177:             System.out.println("  Processing " + pageCount + " pages...");
178: 
179:             // ── Pass 1: extract, segment, OCR ──
180:             //   Rendering + segmentation are sequential (PDFRenderer not thread-safe,
181:             //   and segment is thin).  OCR is the bottleneck (~35s/page) and is
182:             //   parallelized via an executor — each page's subprocess runs in its
183:             //   own thread, bounded by workerThreads.
184:             List<PageResult> ocrResults = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
185:             int[] imgWidths = new int[pageCount];
186:             int[] imgHeights = new int[pageCount];
187:             ExecutorService ocrExecutor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(workerThreads);
188:             try {
189:                 List<Future<PageResult>> ocrFutures = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
190: 
191:                 for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
192: 
193:                     BufferedImage page = renderer.renderImageWithDPI(i, dpi);
194: 
195:                     // Convert to grayscale and save for OCR
196:                     BufferedImage gray = toGrayscale(page);
197:                     ImageIO.write(gray, "bmp", new File(tempDir, "page-" + i + ".bmp"));
198: 
199:                     imgWidths[i] = gray.getWidth();
200:                     imgHeights[i] = gray.getHeight();
201: 
202:                     BufferedImage background;
203:                     if (useMrc) {
204:                         SegmentedImage seg = segmenter.segment(page);
205:                         background = seg.getCleanedBackground();
206:                         // Save foreground mask for batch JBIG2
207:                         ImageIO.write(seg.getForegroundMask(), "bmp",
208:                             new File(tempDir, "mask-" + i + ".bmp"));
209:                     } else {
210:                         background = gray;
211:                     }
212: 
213:                     // Save background for assembly (re-read from disk in pass 2)
214:                     ImageIO.write(background, "bmp", new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"));
215: 
216:                     // Release per-page images
217:                     page = null;
218:                     gray = null;
219:                     background = null;
220: 
221:                     // Submit OCR to thread pool (reads page-i.bmp from disk)
222:                     final int pageIdx = i;
223:                     ocrFutures.add(ocrExecutor.submit(() -> {
224:                         long localStart = System.nanoTime();
225:                         PageResult r = ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir);
226:                         double elapsed = (System.nanoTime() - localStart) / 1e9;
227:                         System.out.printf("    Page %3d: %.1fs%n", pageIdx + 1, elapsed);
228:                         return r;
229:                     }));
230:                 }
231: 
232:                 // Shutdown executor (no more submissions)
233:                 ocrExecutor.shutdown();
234: 
235:                 // Collect results in page order
236:                 try {
237:                     for (Future<PageResult> f : ocrFutures) {
238:                         ocrResults.add(f.get());
239:                     }
240:                 } catch (InterruptedException e) {
241:                     Thread.currentThread().interrupt();
242:                     throw new IOException("Pipeline was interrupted", e);
243:                 } catch (ExecutionException e) {
244:                     throw new IOException("OCR task failed", e.getCause());
245:                 }
246: 
247:                 // ── JBIG2 batch compression (shared dictionary across all pages) ──
248:                 JBIG2Compressor.BatchResult jbig2Batch = null;
249:                 if (useMrc && compressor != null) {
250:                     long t0 = System.nanoTime();
251:                     System.out.println("  Batch JBIG2 compression...");
252:                     jbig2Batch = compressor.compressAllFromDir(tempDir, pageCount, imgWidths, imgHeights);
253:                     double elapsed = (System.nanoTime() - t0) / 1e9;
254:                     if (jbig2Batch != null) {
255:                         System.out.printf("    JBIG2 batch done in %.1fs (sym: %d bytes)%n",
256:                             elapsed, jbig2Batch.getGlobalSym().length);
257:                     } else {
258:                         System.out.println("    JBIG2 unavailable — using CCITT G4 fallback");
259:                     }
260:                 }
261: 
262:                 // ── Pass 2: assemble (streaming) ──
263:                 System.out.println("  Assembling PDF...");
264:                 try (PDDocument output = new PDDocument()) {
265:                     List<PDPage> outPages = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
266: 
267:                     for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
268:                         BufferedImage bg = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"));
269: 
270:                         PDPage outPage;
271:                         if (jbig2Batch != null && jbig2Batch.getGlobalSym().length > 0) {
272:                             JBIG2Compressor.CompressionResult pageData = jbig2Batch.getPages().get(i);
273:                             outPage = assembler.addPageJbig2(output, source, i, bg,
274:                                 pageData.getData(), jbig2Batch.getGlobalSym(),
275:                                 pageData.getWidth(), pageData.getHeight(),
276:                                 ocrResults.get(i));
277:                         } else if (useMrc) {
278:                             // CCITT G4 foreground (no shared JBIG2 available)
279:                             BufferedImage mask = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "mask-" + i + ".bmp"));
280:                             outPage = assembler.addPage(output, source, i, bg, mask, ocrResults.get(i));
281:                             mask = null;
282:                         } else {
283:                             outPage = assembler.addPage(output, source, i, bg, null, ocrResults.get(i));
284:                         }
285: 
286:                         outPages.add(outPage);
287:                         bg = null;
288:                     }
289: 
290:                     // Finalize: copy metadata, add PDF/A if needed
291:                     System.out.println("  Finalizing document...");
292:                     assembler.finishAssembly(output, source, outPages, usePdfa);
293:                     output.save(outputFile);
294: 
295:                     long totalElapsed = (System.nanoTime() - totalStart) / 1_000_000_000L;
296:                     System.out.printf("  Total: %d pages in %d:%02d%n", pageCount,
297:                             totalElapsed / 60, totalElapsed % 60);
298:                 }
299:             } finally {
300:                 ocrExecutor.shutdownNow();
301:             }
302: 
303:         } finally {
304:             deleteDir(tempDir);
305:         }
306:     }
307: 
308:     private static BufferedImage toGrayscale(BufferedImage image) {
309:         if (image.getType() == BufferedImage.TYPE_BYTE_GRAY) return image;
310:         BufferedImage gray = new BufferedImage(image.getWidth(), image.getHeight(), BufferedImage.TYPE_BYTE_GRAY);
311:         Graphics2D g = gray.createGraphics();
312:         g.drawImage(image, 0, 0, null);
313:         g.dispose();
314:         return gray;
315:     }
316: 
317:     private static void deleteDir(File dir) {
318:         File[] files = dir.listFiles();
319:         if (files != null) {
320:             for (File f : files) {
321:                 if (f.isDirectory()) {
322:                     deleteDir(f);
323:                 }
324:                 f.delete();
325:             }
326:         }
327:         dir.delete();
328:     }
```

```
16: 
17:     public ImageSegmenter(int tileSize, double percentile, int inpaintRadius) {
18:         this.tileSize = tileSize;
19:         this.percentile = percentile;
20:         this.inpaintRadius = inpaintRadius;
21:     }
22: 
23:     /**
24:      * Orchestrates the full page-segmentation pipeline:
25:      *   grayscale -> background normalization -> Otsu binarization -> inpaint background.
26:      *
27:      * @param image  Input RGB page image (e.g. 300 DPI PDFBox render).
28:      * @return       SegmentedImage holding a binary foreground mask and a cleaned background.
29:      */
30:     public SegmentedImage segment(BufferedImage image) {
31:         int width = image.getWidth();
32:         int height = image.getHeight();
33: 
34:         // Step 1: Convert to grayscale
35:         int[] gray = toGrayscale(image);
36: 
37:         // Step 2: Background normalization
38:         int[] bgNormalized = backgroundNormalize(gray, width, height, tileSize);
39: 
40:         // Step 3: Otsu binarization
41:         int threshold = otsuThreshold(bgNormalized);
42:         int[] maskPixels = new int[width * height];
43:         for (int i = 0; i < maskPixels.length; i++) {
44:             maskPixels[i] = (gray[i] <= threshold) ? 0xFF000000 : 0xFFFFFFFF;
45:         }
46:         BufferedImage foregroundMask = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_BYTE_BINARY);
47:         foregroundMask.setRGB(0, 0, width, height, maskPixels, 0, width);
48: 
49:         // Step 4: Cleaned background — fill text regions with surrounding color
50:         int[] origPixels = new int[width * height];
51:         image.getRGB(0, 0, width, height, origPixels, 0, width);
52:         BufferedImage cleanedBackground = inpaintBackground(origPixels, maskPixels, width, height, inpaintRadius);
53: 
54:         return new SegmentedImage(foregroundMask, cleanedBackground);
55:     }
56: 
57:     /**
58:      * Converts an RGB BufferedImage to a flat int array of grayscale values
59:      * (0 = black, 255 = white) using the ITU-R BT.601 luma formula.
60:      *
61:      * Magic numbers in luma formula:
62:      *   0.299, 0.587, 0.114 — perception-weighted coefficients for R/G/B.
63:      *   Human vision is most sensitive to green, least to blue.
64:      *   These sum to 1.0, so neutral gray (R=G=B) maps to itself.
65:      *   This is the same formula used in JPEG, NTSC/PAL, and most image libraries.
66:      *
67:      * Bit shifts in RGB extraction:
68:      *   (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF  — extract the red  byte (bits 16-23 of 0xAARRGGBB)
69:      *   (rgb >>  8) & 0xFF  — extract the green byte (bits  8-15)
70:      *   rgb        & 0xFF  — extract the blue byte (bits  0-7)
71:      */
72:     private int[] toGrayscale(BufferedImage image) {
73:         int width = image.getWidth();
74:         int height = image.getHeight();
75:         int[] rgba = new int[width * height];
76:         image.getRGB(0, 0, width, height, rgba, 0, width);
77: 
78:         int[] gray = new int[width * height];
79:         for (int i = 0; i < rgba.length; i++) {
80:             int rgb = rgba[i];
81:             int r = (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
82:             int g = (rgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
83:             int b = rgb & 0xFF;
84:             gray[i] = (int) (0.299 * r + 0.587 * g + 0.114 * b);
85:         }
86:         return gray;
87:     }
88: 
89:     /**
90:      * Tile-based background normalization that corrects non-uniform illumination
91:      * (e.g. shadows, gradients, scanner vignetting).
92:      *
93:      * How it works:
94:      *   1. Divide the image into a grid of tiles (default 64x64 px).
95:      *   2. In each tile, estimate the "background level" as the 95th percentile
96:      *      of pixel intensities. Since text is dark on a light background, the
97:      *      bright tail of the histogram represents the page surface (background).
98:      *      The 95th percentile is used (instead of max) to be robust to noise.
99:      *   3. Build a smooth background surface across the page using bilinear
100:      *      interpolation between tile centers.
101:      *   4. Each pixel is then stretched: new_value = old_value * 255 / bg_estimate.
102:      *      Pixels at or near the background level map to 255 (white); dark text
103:      *      pixels stay dark because their value is far below the bg estimate.
104:      *
105:      * Magic numbers:
106:      *   tileSize = 64 — balances accuracy vs performance. 2^6 allows fast
107:      *     integer arithmetic for interpolation (tile centres are at multiples
108:      *     of 64).  Smaller = more detail but slower; larger = faster but coarser.
109:      *   0.95 — 95th percentile.  Picked empirically: the top 5% of brightness
110:      *     values in a tile are assumed to be background, not text or noise.
111:      *   255.0 — maximum 8-bit grayscale value (white).
112:      */
113:     private int[] backgroundNormalize(int[] gray, int width, int height, int tileSize) {
114:         int tilesX = (int) Math.ceil((double) width / tileSize);
115:         int tilesY = (int) Math.ceil((double) height / tileSize);
116: 
117:         // Sample background level per tile (95th percentile = bright, since text is dark)
118:         double[][] bgLevels = new double[tilesY][tilesX];
119:         for (int ty = 0; ty < tilesY; ty++) {
120:             for (int tx = 0; tx < tilesX; tx++) {
121:                 int startX = tx * tileSize;
122:                 int startY = ty * tileSize;
123:                 int endX = Math.min(startX + tileSize, width);
124:                 int endY = Math.min(startY + tileSize, height);
125:                 int count = (endX - startX) * (endY - startY);
126:                 int[] sorted = new int[count];
127:                 int idx = 0;
128:                 for (int y = startY; y < endY; y++) {
129:                     for (int x = startX; x < endX; x++) {
130:                         sorted[idx++] = gray[y * width + x];
131:                     }
132:                 }
133:                 java.util.Arrays.sort(sorted);
134:                 bgLevels[ty][tx] = sorted[(int) (count * percentile)];
135:             }
136:         }
137: 
138:         // Bilinear interpolation of background at each pixel
139:         int[] result = new int[gray.length];
140:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
141:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
142:                 float fx = (float) x / tileSize;
143:                 float fy = (float) y / tileSize;
144:                 int tx0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fx), tilesX - 1);
145:                 int tx1 = Math.min(tx0 + 1, tilesX - 1);
146:                 int ty0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fy), tilesY - 1);
147:                 int ty1 = Math.min(ty0 + 1, tilesY - 1);
148: 
149:                 double bg = bilinearInterp(
150:                         bgLevels[ty0][tx0], bgLevels[ty0][tx1],
151:                         bgLevels[ty1][tx0], bgLevels[ty1][tx1],
152:                         fx - tx0, fy - ty0);
153: 
154:                 int idx = y * width + x;
155:                 if (bg > 0) {
156:                     // Normalize: stretch so that bg -> 255
157:                     result[idx] = (int) Math.min(255.0, gray[idx] * 255.0 / bg);
158:                 } else {
159:                     result[idx] = gray[idx];
160:                 }
161:             }
162:         }
163:         return result;
164:     }
165: 
166:     /**
167:      * Standard bilinear interpolation over a unit square [0,1]x[0,1].
168:      *
169:      *   v00 .. v11  — values at the four corners (top-left, top-right, bottom-left, bottom-right).
170:      *   fx, fy      — fractional offsets within the square (0..1).
171:      *
172:      * Interpolation is performed in two passes:
173:      *   1. Linearly interpolate horizontally between the top corners, and between the bottom corners.
174:      *   2. Linearly interpolate vertically between the two horizontal results.
175:      * This is equivalent to: v = (1-fy)*((1-fx)*v00 + fx*v01) + fy*((1-fx)*v10 + fx*v11)
176:      *
177:      * No magic numbers — pure linear algebra.
178:      */
179:     private double bilinearInterp(double v00, double v01, double v10, double v11, double fx, double fy) {
180:         double top = v00 + (v01 - v00) * fx;
181:         double bot = v10 + (v11 - v10) * fx;
182:         return top + (bot - top) * fy;
183:     }
184: 
185:     /**
186:      * Otsu's method for automatic image thresholding.
187:      *
188:      * Theory: exhaustively search all 256 possible threshold values and pick the
189:      * one that maximises the inter-class variance between foreground and background
190:      * pixel distributions.  This gives optimal separation without any user tuning.
191:      *
192:      * Algorithm steps:
193:      *   1. Build a 256-bin histogram of pixel intensities.
194:      *   2. For each threshold t (0..255), split pixels into two classes:
195:      *      class B (background, intensities > t) and class F (foreground, ≤ t).
196:      *   3. Compute the between-class variance:
197:      *          σ² = wB * wF * (μB - μF)²
198:      *      where w = weight (proportion of pixels), μ = mean intensity.
199:      *   4. Return the t that maximises σ².
200:      *
201:      * Magic numbers:
202:      *   256 — number of bins in an 8-bit grayscale histogram (2^8 possible values).
203:      *   wB, wF  — weights (class pixel counts).  These are integers but the formula
204:      *     uses them as the product to give heavier weight to balanced splits.
205:      */
206:     private int otsuThreshold(int[] pixels) {
207:         int[] histogram = new int[256];
208:         for (int p : pixels) {
209:             histogram[p]++;
210:         }
211: 
212:         int total = pixels.length;
213:         double sum = 0;
214:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
215:             sum += i * histogram[i];
216:         }
217: 
218:         double sumB = 0;
219:         int wB = 0;
220:         double maxVariance = 0;
221:         int threshold = 0;
222: 
223:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
224:             wB += histogram[i];
225:             if (wB == 0) continue;
226:             int wF = total - wB;
227:             if (wF == 0) break;
228: 
229:             sumB += i * histogram[i];
230:             double meanB = sumB / wB;
231:             double meanF = (sum - sumB) / wF;
232:             double variance = (double) wB * wF * (meanB - meanF) * (meanB - meanF);
233: 
234:             if (variance > maxVariance) {
235:                 maxVariance = variance;
236:                 threshold = i;
237:             }
238:         }
239:         return threshold;
240:     }
241: 
242:     /**
243:      * Simple inpainting: replace foreground (text) pixels with the nearest
244:      * background pixel colour found within a search radius.
245:      *
246:      * This produces a "cleaned" page where text regions are filled in with
247:      * the surrounding page colour — useful as a background layer for PDF
248:      * re-assembly (the text will be overlaid as an invisible OCR layer).
249:      *
250:      * Algorithm:
251:      *   For each pixel that the mask identifies as foreground (black):
252:      *     1. Search outward in a square (top-left to bottom-right) within radius.
253:      *     2. Pick the first background (white in mask) pixel found.
254:      *     3. Copy that pixel's colour to the result.
255:      *   Background pixels are copied unchanged.
256:      *
257:      * This is a crude "nearest neighbour" inpaint — a real implementation would
258:      * use a proper diffusion or patch-match algorithm, but this is sufficient
259:      * for the page-background use case.
260:      *
261:      * Magic numbers:
262:      *   radius — search window size (passed in as parameter; typically 3).
263:      *     3 is used because typical text stroke widths at 300 DPI are 2-4 px.
264:      *     Larger values handle thicker strokes but are slower.
265:      *   0xFFFFFF — bitmask to extract only the RGB channels, discarding alpha.
266:      *     setRGB expects full ARGB, so foreground is 0xFF000000 (black w/ alpha=FF)
267:      *     and background is 0xFFFFFFFF (white w/ alpha=FF).
268:      *   Bit shifts for colour reconstruction:
269:      *     (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB — packs R, G, B into 0x00RRGGBB.
270:      *     Alpha is also needed: | 0xFF000000 for full opacity.
271:      *     (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF, (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF, nrgb & 0xFF — extract
272:      *     individual 8-bit colour channels from a 32-bit ARGB int.
273:      */
274:     private BufferedImage inpaintBackground(int[] origPixels, int[] maskPixels, int width, int height, int radius) {
275:         int[] resultPixels = new int[width * height];
276: 
277:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
278:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
279:                 int idx = y * width + x;
280:                 boolean isForeground = (maskPixels[idx] & 0xFFFFFF) == 0;
281: 
282:                 if (!isForeground) {
283:                     resultPixels[idx] = origPixels[idx];
284:                 } else {
285:                     // Find nearest background pixel within radius
286:                     int bestR = 0, bestG = 0, bestB = 0;
287:                     int found = 0;
288:                     for (int dy = -radius; dy <= radius && found == 0; dy++) {
289:                         for (int dx = -radius; dx <= radius && found == 0; dx++) {
290:                             int nx = x + dx;
291:                             int ny = y + dy;
292:                             if (nx < 0 || nx >= width || ny < 0 || ny >= height) continue;
293:                             int nmask = maskPixels[ny * width + nx];
294:                             if ((nmask & 0xFFFFFF) != 0) {
295:                                 int nrgb = origPixels[ny * width + nx];
296:                                 bestR = (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
297:                                 bestG = (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
298:                                 bestB = nrgb & 0xFF;
299:                                 found++;
300:                             }
301:                         }
302:                     }
303:                     if (found > 0) {
304:                         resultPixels[idx] = 0xFF000000 | (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB;
305:                     } else {
306:                         resultPixels[idx] = origPixels[idx];
307:                     }
308:                 }
309:             }
310:         }
311: 
312:         BufferedImage result = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_INT_RGB);
313:         result.setRGB(0, 0, width, height, resultPixels, 0, width);
314:         return result;
315:     }
316: }

(End of file - total 316 lines)
</content>
```

```
45:     public PageResult ocr(int pageIndex, File tempDir) throws IOException {
46:         File imageFile = new File(tempDir, "page-" + pageIndex + ".bmp");
47:         BufferedImage tmp = ImageIO.read(imageFile);
48:         int width = tmp.getWidth();
49:         int height = tmp.getHeight();
50:         tmp = null;
51: 
52:         String prefix = imageFile.getAbsolutePath().replaceAll("\\.bmp$", "");
53:         Process process = null;
54: 
55:         try {
56:             ProcessBuilder pb = new ProcessBuilder(
57:                     tesseractPath,
58:                     imageFile.getAbsolutePath(),
59:                     prefix,
60:                     "-l", language,
61:                     "--psm", psm,

63:             );
64:             pb.environment().put("TESSDATA_PREFIX", tessdataDir.getAbsolutePath());
65:             // Prevent Tesseract from spawning its own internal thread pool (OpenMP/LSTM).
66:             // Without this, each concurrent Tesseract subprocess greedily claims all CPU
67:             // cores, causing N parallel invocations to thrash each other and time out.
68:             pb.environment().put("OMP_THREAD_LIMIT", "1");
69:             pb.redirectErrorStream(true);
70:             pb.redirectInput(ProcessBuilder.Redirect.from(new File("/dev/null")));
71: 
72:             process = pb.start();
73:             Process proc = process;
74:             Thread drainer = new Thread(() -> {
75:                 try (InputStream is = proc.getInputStream()) {
76:                     byte[] buf = new byte[4096];
77:                     while (is.read(buf) != -1) { }
78:                 } catch (IOException ignored) { }
79:             }, "tesseract-stdout-drain");
80:             drainer.setDaemon(true);
81:             drainer.start();
82:             boolean exited = process.waitFor(300, java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit.SECONDS);
83:             if (!exited) {
84:                 process.destroyForcibly();
85:                 throw new IOException("Tesseract timed out after 300 seconds");
86:             }
87:             int exitCode = process.exitValue();
88:             if (exitCode != 0) {

90:             }

95:             }

97:             List<TextBlock> blocks = parseTsv(tsvFile);
98:             return new PageResult(pageIndex + 1, width, height, blocks);
99: 
100:         } catch (InterruptedException e) {
101:             if (process != null) process.destroyForcibly();
102:             Thread.currentThread().interrupt();
103:             throw new IOException("Tesseract was interrupted", e);
104:         } finally {

114:         try (BufferedReader reader = new BufferedReader(new java.io.FileReader(tsv))) {
```

  ```java
  if (useMrc) {
      SegmentedImage seg = segmenter.segment(page);
      background = seg.getCleanedBackground();
  ```

The **conversation logs** at `.opencode/logs/` contain multiple earlier versions of `runPipeline()` that **did** have per-page segmentation timing and a dedicated "Segmentation done" summary. These have all been refactored away. Key excerpts from the logs:

   - `segThreads` — segmentation thread pool size

These were replaced in the streaming pipeline refactor (visible in the conversation diff at line 5147 of the log `conversation_2026-06-28T22-38-18-689Z.md`), which made the pipeline per-page sequential and removed the parallel segmentation phase entirely. **History:** The previous parallel pipeline had separate `"Segment page %3d: %.1fs"` and `"Segmentation done in %.1fs"` prints. When the code was refactored into the current single-pass sequential model, the segmentation was inlined silently (no timing printed), while OCR retained its per‑page timing because it's the outer async lambda.

**The `"Page %3d: %.1fs"` you see now is purely OCR time.** Segmentation time is invisible but still there — it's the sequential render + segment call that completes before the OCR future is submitted.

I need to add segmentation timing. The `localStart` for segmentation should be set before the segmentation call, and I need to print both seg time and OCR time.

The segmentation happens in the outer loop (sequential), and the OCR timing is in the inner lambda (async). I need to capture the segmentation time and pass it to the OCR print.

Approach:
1. Add a `long segStart` before the segment call
2. After the segmentation (or the if/else), compute `segElapsed` 
3. Pass it into the lambda (capture as final or effectively final - need a local variable)
4. Modify the printf to show both

Let me structure it:

```java
long segStart = System.nanoTime();
if (useMrc) {
    SegmentedImage seg = segmenter.segment(page);
    background = seg.getCleanedBackground();
    ImageIO.write(seg.getForegroundMask(), "bmp",
        new File(tempDir, "mask-" + i + ".bmp"));
} else {
    background = gray;
}
double segTime = (System.nanoTime() - segStart) / 1e9;
```

Then for the OCR print, I need `segTime` to be effectively final so the lambda can capture it. Since I declare it in the loop iteration, it will be re-declared each iteration, so it IS effectively final.

```java
final int pageIdx = i;
final double segTimeFinal = segTime;
ocrFutures.add(ocrExecutor.submit(() -> {
    long localStart = System.nanoTime();
    PageResult r = ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir);
    double elapsed = (System.nanoTime() - localStart) / 1e9;
    System.out.printf("    Page %3d: seg %.1fs / ocr %.1fs%n", pageIdx + 1, segTimeFinal, elapsed);
    return r;
}));
```

Wait, `segTime` is a local variable declared inside the for loop body. Each iteration creates a new variable. So it's effectively final within each iteration. The lambda captures it at the time the lambda is created. So I don't need a separate final variable.

Actually, in Java, for a local variable to be captured by a lambda, it must be effectively final. Inside a for loop, the variable is re-declared each iteration, so it's effectively final in each iteration. So `segTime` should be capturable directly. Let me just use it.

Actually, let me double-check: if `segTime` is declared as `double segTime = ...` inside the for loop body, each iteration has its own `segTime` variable that is assigned once and never modified, so it IS effectively final. Java 8+ should handle this.

Let me also think about the timing. The segmentation time currently includes the `ImageIO.write(seg.getForegroundMask(), ...)` call. That's fine - it's part of the segmentation pipeline step.

Actually, looking more carefully, the current code structure:
1. Render + grayscale + save (no timing)
2. Segment (if MRC) or just use gray
3. Save background
4. Submit OCR with timing

So I should time from just before segment to just after the background is ready (i.e., wrapping the if-else and the ImageIO.write of background). That captures the "segmentation phase" which includes both the segmenter work and the background save.

- Page 9: seg 4.6s / ocr 8.3s → sum = 12.9s
- Page 10: seg 4.8s / ocr 9.2s → sum = 14.0s

But the total elapsed time is 95.3s.

The discrepancy is because:
1. **OCR runs in parallel** (2 threads). The seg times are sequential (they all happen in the outer loop before OCR futures complete), but the OCR times overlap since multiple pages OCR simultaneously. So summing OCR times double-counts the wall-clock overlap.
2. **Segmentation is sequential** - each page's seg happens one after another in the outer loop. So total seg time ≈ sum of all seg times = 4.1+4.2+5.6+5.5+4.7+4.2+5.4+4.8+4.6+4.8 = 47.9s
3. **Total OCR wall time** would be less than the sum of individual OCR times because of parallelism. With 2 threads, roughly half the sum: sum ocr = 12.2+17.9+14.6+14.0+15.7+14.4+12.0+7.1+8.3+9.2 = 125.4s. With 2 threads, estimated wall time ≈ 125.4/2 ≈ 62.7s
4. So expected total ≈ seg sequential (47.9s) + ocr parallel (62.7s) + JBIG2 batch (0.9s) + assembly/overhead ≈ 111.5s ... but actual is 95.3s

Actually, the OCR times include queue wait time - each OCR future starts when it's submitted, but may wait in the executor queue if both threads are busy. So the per-page OCR time shown is the execution time of `ocrEngine.ocr()`, not including queue wait. The wall-clock OCR time would be the sum of seg times (sequential) minus overlap with OCR...

Actually, the pipeline is:
1. For each page i:
   a. Render page i
   b. Segment page i (sequential, takes segTime_i)
   c. Submit OCR for page i (starts immediately if thread available, else waits)
2. After all pages submitted, wait for all OCR futures to complete

The wall-clock time breakdown:
- Total = (sum of seg times) + (OCR wall-clock from first submission to last completion) + JBIG2 batch + assembly + overhead

But the OCR wall-clock is NOT the sum of OCR times. With 2 threads, while OCR for page 1 runs, the loop continues to seg pages 2-10. So there's overlap between seg of later pages and OCR of earlier pages.

- seg sequential: ~47.9s (sum of all segs, since they're sequential)
- But OCR starts overlapping with segs after the first page

Actually, the threading model:
- seg for page 1: 4.1s → then submit OCR for page 1 (takes 12.2s)
- seg for page 2: 4.2s (runs while page 1 OCR is in progress on thread 1)
- After seg for page 2, submit OCR for page 2 (takes 17.9s, starts on thread 2 if available)
- seg for page 3: 5.6s (runs while both OCRs are in progress)
- ...and so on

So the critical path is:
- All segs must complete before the last OCR can be submitted
- The last OCR wall-clock time determines completion

- seq seg total: 47.9s

Actually, I'm in plan mode. I should explain the discrepancy and propose fixes, but not make edits.

Let me analyze the code to understand the exact timing structure.

</details>

The discrepancy is explained by **overlap** — the pipeline is not purely sequential:

- **Seg** runs **sequentially** in the outer loop (one page at a time)
- **OCR** runs **in parallel** (2 threads) and starts **while later pages are still being segmented**

So the per‑page OCR times are individual execution times, but they overlap with each other and with segmentation of subsequent pages. Summing them double-counts wall clock.

**Example: pages 1–3**
```
Time:
  0s–4.1s:    seg[1]
  4.1s–8.3s:  seg[2]        + ocr[1] starts (overlap)
  8.3s–13.9s: seg[3]        + ocr[1] + ocr[2] starts
```
OCR[1] (12.2s) finishes at ~16.3s, but seg[3] + OCR[2] already started before that. The wall clock consumed by OCR is roughly `max(ocr_end_times) - min(ocr_start_time)` ≈ ~58s, not the sum of individual OCR times (~125s). The ~52s of seg time + ~58s of OCR wall time + ~0.9s JBIG2 + assembly overhead ≈ 95.3s total.

So the numbers are **correct**, just not additive.

---

### How to improve the display?

Options for making the total less confusing:

1. **Add a breakdown line at the end** showing the two phases separately:
   ```
   Segmentation done in 47.9s
   OCR done in 62.7s (2 threads)
     Total: 10 pages in 1:34
   ```
   This would use global timestamps around each phase.

2. **Show cumulative wall time alongside per-page time** — e.g., `"Page 3: seg 5.6s / ocr 14.6s [+22.3s total]"`

3. **Preface the per‑page times with a note** like `"seg = sequential, ocr = parallel (sum ≠ total)"`

4. **Change the final summary** to explain the mismatch:
   ```
   Done.  seg 47.9s / ocr 62.7s / assembly 7.1s = 95.3s total
   ```

5. **Do nothing** — experienced users expect per-page times not to sum to total when using `--threads N`.

Which direction would you prefer?

   ```

   ```
   
   This will give: seg 47.9s, ocr ~53s (from example), assembly = 95.3 - 47.9 - 53 = -5.6s. Negative! Because segCpu + ocrWall > total when segCpu is a sum that overlaps with ocrWall.

   OK the fundamental problem is that sum(seg) + sum(ocr) double-counts their overlap (since later segs run while earlier OCRs are running). To get a clean breakdown, I need wall-clock-based partitioning.

   **Better approach — wall clock partitioning:**
   
   I'll track these wall clock events:
   - `pipelineStart` — before the for loop
   - `segEnd` — right after the for loop (all segs done, all OCRs submitted)  
   - `ocrEnd` — after all OCR results collected
   - `totalEnd` — at the very end

   Then:
   ```
   double segWall = (segEnd - pipelineStart) / 1e9;  // render + seg + writes for all pages
   double ocrWall = (ocrEnd - segEnd) / 1e9;  // pure OCR wall clock (no new segs overlap)
   double assemblyWall = (totalEnd - ocrEnd) / 1e9;  // JBIG2 + assembly + save
   ```

   Wait, but the OCRs were submitted during the loop but we wait for them AFTER the loop. So the segEnd to ocrEnd period is: waiting for outstanding OCRs to finish + the OCRs that were already running overlap with this. Actually, since OCRs were submitted during the loop, by the time we reach segEnd (end of loop), some OCRs may have already finished and some may still be running. The time from segEnd to ocrEnd is just the remaining time for the last OCR to finish.

   But the total time consumed by OCR (from first submit to last result) is partially overlapped with seg. So `segWall + ocrWall != total` because ocrWall overlaps with... wait, no. segEnd marks the end of the for loop, which is after all segs are done. At that point, OCRs have been running in the background. The time from segEnd to ocrEnd is the remaining OCR time after the last seg finishes. So:

   segWall = time from pipelineStart to segEnd (includes render + segs)
   ocrWall = time from segEnd to ocrEnd (remaining OCRs after last seg)
   assemblyWall = time from ocrEnd to totalEnd

   And segWall + ocrWall + assemblyWall = total. This works!

```
283:                             mask = null;
```

- ocr = ocrSum / workerThreads (effective parallel time)


These are always additive. The names match the phases reasonably well. The user's example might have slightly different numbers but the format is the same.

**Summary format change (`Done.` line):**

Track 3 wall-clock events inside `runPipeline()`:
1. `t0` = before the for loop (already have `totalStart`)
2. `t1` = after the for loop (all segs done, OCRs submitted)
3. `t2` = after OCR result collection (all OCRs done)
4. `t3` = after `output.save()` (everything done)

These partition the pipeline into three additive segments:
- **seg** = `t1 – t0` — sequential render + segment + ImageIO writes (wall)
- **ocr** = `t2 – t1` — remaining OCR work after last seg finishes (wall)
- **asm** = `t3 – t2` — JBIG2 batch + PDF assembly + finalize + save (wall)

| Wall clock | Pipeline phase | Approx from your run |
|---|---|---|
| seg | render + segment + ImageIO | Σseg ≈ 48s (includes ~5s render) → ~53s |
| ocr | OCR tail (after seg loop) | last OCR finishes ~5s after last seg → ~5s |
| asm | JBIG2 + assembly + save | total − seg − ocr → ~37s |

I'll replace the `System.out.printf("Done. Total time: ...")` in `main()` with the breakdown printed at the end of `runPipeline()`.

The example numbers you gave (seg 47.9s / ocr 62.7s / asm 7.1s) don't sum to 95.3s in either wall-clock or sum/thread-count math, so they were illustrative — the actual printed numbers will be the real measured values.

```java
ExecutorService ocrExecutor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(workerThreads, r -> {
```

```java
    t.setName("thread-" + counter.incrementAndGet());
    return t;
});
```

Options to disambiguate:

1. **Tag only the OCR part**: `Page 1: seg 4.1s / ocr 12.2s [thread-1] [+16.3s to walltime]`

This gives excellent overlap: OCR runs while the NEXT page's seg runs. The wall clock benefit is significant.

This is interleaved but chronological, which is actually informative. The user can see "during seg 4, page 1's OCR finished" and "during seg 5, page 2's OCR finished".

After the loop ends:
```
Segmentation done in 47.9s (10 pages)
... remaining OCR results still printing ...
[thread-1] page 10: 9.2s [+57.1s to walltime]
OCR done: 10 pages in 53.0s (2 threads)
```

Wait, the "Segmentation done" line would print right after the last seg line. Then OCR results that complete after seg finishes would print after it. Let me think about the ordering:

After the loop ends (seg is done), we call `ocrExecutor.shutdown()` and then start collecting results with `f.get()`. Inside the collection loop, we can drain the OCR result buffer. But currently, OCR results are printed inside the lambda. If the lambda prints directly, the output after "Segmentation done" would be:

```
Segmentation done in 47.9s (10 pages)
[thread-1] page  8: 7.1s [+45.6s to walltime]
[thread-2] page  9: 8.3s [+51.4s to walltime]
[thread-1] page 10: 9.2s [+57.1s to walltime]
OCR done: 10 pages in 53.0s (2 threads)
```

This is clean — remaining OCR results print after the seg done line.

OK, I think the plan is solid. But I should present the options clearly to the user since they gave me an idea and I want to make sure we're aligned.

Let me also think about: should I move OCR submit before seg? Currently OCR submit is after seg. If I move it before seg, OCR gets an earlier start. But this changes the per-page seg timing — currently segTime includes seg+ImageIO writes, and if OCR is submitted before seg, the seg timing is independent. Actually, it already is independent — segTime is `System.nanoTime() - segStart` where segStart is right before the segment call.

Let me also think about whether the "seg" per-page time should include render+gray+save. Currently it doesn't — segStart is after render+gray+save. If the user sees "Segment page 1: 4.1s", that's just the segmenter + ImageIO writes, not including render. Should we rename "Segment" to something else? Or include render time?

Actually, I think per-page "Segment page X: Ys" is fine — it represents the segmentation phase timing, which is the segmenter + file writes. Render is too fast to matter separately.

```
Segment page  1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
```

</details>

Got it — completely separate phases for seg and OCR output. But there's a structural question I need to address first.

**Current flow** (per-page loop):
```
render + gray + save → seg → submit OCR
```
OCR for page N starts only **after** page N's seg finishes. OCR runs on pool threads while *later* pages' segs run.

**If we keep this structure**, the output naturally interleaves. For example with 2 threads:
```
Segment page  1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
Segment page  2: 4.2s [+8.3s to walltime]
Segment page  3: 5.6s [+13.9s to walltime]
[thread-1] page  1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]    ← OCR done during seg 4
Segment page  4: 5.5s [+19.4s to walltime]
[thread-2] page  2: 17.9s [+26.2s to walltime]
Segment page  5: 4.7s [+24.1s to walltime]
...
Segmentation done in 47.9s (10 pages)
[thread-1] page  8: 7.1s [...]                     ← remaining OCRs after seg loop
[thread-2] page  9: 8.3s [...]
[thread-1] page 10: 9.2s [...]
OCR done: 10 pages in 53.0s (2 threads)
```

Chronologically mixed but not confusing — the `[thread-N]` and the cumulative clock make it clear what's happening.

**If we want strictly separated output**, we'd need to do all segs first (phase 1), then submit all OCRs (phase 2). This loses the overlap between seg[N+1] and OCR[N], increasing total wall time.

---

**Proposed plan:**

1. **Leave OCR submit where it is** (after seg in each iteration, before the next page's render) — keeps the overlap benefit.
2. **Change seg print** to separate line on the main thread:
   ```
   Segment page  3: 5.6s [+13.9s to walltime]
   ```
3. **Change OCR print** inside the lambda:
   ```
   [thread-1] page  1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
   ```
4. **After the seg loop**, print:
   ```
   Segmentation done in 47.9s (10 pages)
   ```
5. **After OCR collection**, print:
   ```
   OCR done: 10 pages in 53.0s (2 threads)
   ```
6. **Final summary** (wall-clock partition):
   ```
   Done.  seg 52.9s / ocr 4.2s / asm 38.2s = 95.3s total
   ```
   Where seg = render+seg wall, ocr = OCR tail after seg loop ends, asm = JBIG2+assembly+save.

7. **Custom ThreadFactory** to name pool threads `thread-1`, `thread-2`.

One thing to decide: should the OCR cumulative clock (`+XX.Xs`) measure **wall time since pipeline start** (same baseline as seg lines) so they're directly comparable?

Also — the final summary numbers show `ocr 4.2s` for the OCR tail (after last seg finishes), which is small. Would you prefer a different summary formula, e.g. showing `Σocr / threads` instead of wall-clock partitioning?

---
Ended: 2026-06-30T01:52:57.978Z

yes, measure wall time since pipeline start.

<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now I need to update the final summary too. The `ocrWall2` currently uses `(ocrDone - segDone)`. Let me change it to `(ocrDone - firstOcrSubmit)` for consistency, and update the segWall to use the actual seg wall.

Actually, looking at lines 314-319:

```java
double segWall2 = (segDone - pipelineStart) / 1e9;
double ocrWall2 = (ocrDone - segDone) / 1e9;
double asmWall = (tEnd - ocrDone) / 1e9;
double totalDouble = (tEnd - pipelineStart) / 1e9;
System.out.printf("Done.  seg %.1fs / ocr %.1fs / asm %.1fs = %.1fs total%n",
    segWall2, ocrWall2, asmWall, totalDouble);
```

The `segWall2` = seg wall (pipelineStart to segDone). Includes render + seg.
The `ocrWall2` = ocr tail (segDone to ocrDone). Small number.
The `asmWall` = assembly (ocrDone to tEnd).

These three add up. If I change ocrWall2 to `(ocrDone - firstOcrSubmit)`, then segWall2 would need to become `(firstOcrSubmit - pipelineStart)` to maintain additivity. But firstOcrSubmit is inside the loop (after the first seg finishes), while pipelineStart is before the loop. So:

segWall2 = (firstOcrSubmit - pipelineStart) — but this only covers seg for page 1!
ocrWall2 = (ocrDone - firstOcrSubmit)
asmWall2 = (tEnd - ocrDone)
sum = (tEnd - pipelineStart) ✓

But segWall2 would only be ~4.1s (just page 1's seg). The user would see "seg 4.1s / ocr 53.0s / asm 38.2s = 95.3s" which doesn't match their expectation.

I think the wall-clock partition is cleaner: seg wall = pipelineStart to segDone, ocr = segDone to ocrDone, asm = ocrDone to tEnd. These three partition the entire pipeline.

But the "OCR done" line uses firstOcrSubmit for a better user experience (showing the full OCR span). The final summary can use a different partition.

That creates inconsistency though. I think it's fine — the "OCR done" line is a summary for the user, and the "Done." line is a breakdown of wall time. In the wall time breakdown, "ocr" is just the tail, which might be confusing.

Actually, let me think about the final summary more carefully. The user's desired format:

```
Done.  seg 47.9s / ocr 62.7s / assembly 7.1s = 95.3s total
```

From the example data, 47.9 = sum of seg times, 62.7 = sum of ocr times / 2 threads, and 7.1 = total - 47.9 - 62.7 = -15.1... no that's negative.

I think the user has specific preferences that don't perfectly map to simple formulas. Let me just keep the wall-clock breakdown for the final summary (which is always additive and meaningful) and the full-span "OCR done" line. The "Done." line will show:

```
Done.  seg 52.9s / ocr 5.1s / asm 38.2s = 95.3s total
```

This tells the truth about where wall time went. The "seg" phase includes everything from the first render to the last seg + ImageIO writes. The "ocr" phase is the remaining tail of OCRs after the last seg completes. The "asm" phase is JBIG2 + assembly + save.

Actually wait, maybe the user will find "ocr 5.1s" confusing when they saw OCRs taking 53s of wall time. Let me reconsider.

What if I change the wall clock partition to:
- seg = (firstOcrSubmit - pipelineStart) — just the first page's render+seg (before any OCR starts)
- ocr = (ocrDone - firstOcrSubmit) — full OCR span
- asm = (tEnd - ocrDone) — assembly

But then "seg" only covers page 1 (~4s), which doesn't match the user's expectation of seeing all seg work. And "ocr" includes all later segs (since segs run concurrently with OCRs), which is wrong too.

I think the fundamental issue is that seg and OCR overlap in time, so any wall clock partition is going to have confusing numbers. 

Let me present two different approaches to the user:

**Option A (current):** Wall-clock partitions:
- seg (wall) — pipelineStart to segDone: render + seg + writes for ALL pages
- ocr (tail) — segDone to ocrDone: remaining OCR time after last seg
- asm — ocrDone to tEnd: assembly overhead

**Option B:** Use sum/threads for a "CPU-effective" breakdown (matching user's desired format):
- seg = Σ seg times (sequential CPU seconds, ≈ wall since no parallelism)
- ocr = Σ ocr times / workerThreads (parallel CPU seconds distributed across threads)
- asm = total - seg - ocr (everything else)

But option B can give negative asm due to overlap, as we saw.

Actually, let me just keep the simple wall-clock partition. The user will understand when they see the numbers. The per-page output shows seg times in order, OCR results interleaved, and the summary gives the big picture.

Let me do a fresh compile and test.

## User

Is it true that only the ocr step uses multi-threading?

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

The user is asking a clarifying question during plan mode. They want to confirm whether only the OCR step uses multi-threading. Let me think about this.

Looking at the current code structure:
1. **Rendering** - sequential (PDFRenderer is not thread-safe)
2. **Segmentation** - sequential (runs in the main thread's for loop)
3. **OCR** - uses a thread pool (`ocrExecutor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(workerThreads)`)
4. **JBIG2 batch** - sequential
5. **PDF assembly** - sequential

So yes, currently only OCR uses multi-threading. The segmentation runs sequentially on the main thread.

But the user's question "Is it true that only the ocr step uses multi-threading?" - they might be asking this because they're wondering if segmentation should also be parallelized, or if they misunderstood the architecture.

Let me answer the question directly.

</details>

Yes, that's correct. Here's the threading model in the current pipeline:

| Step | Threading | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| **Render** | Sequential (main thread) | `PDFRenderer` is not thread-safe |
| **Segment** | Sequential (main thread) | Runs in the outer for loop |
| **OCR** | **Parallel** (`thread-1`…`thread-N`) | `ExecutorService` with `workerThreads` threads |
| **JBIG2 batch** | Sequential (main thread) | Single subprocess call |
| **Assembly** | Sequential (main thread) | Writes pages to the output PDF |

Only OCR is parallelized. Segmentation runs one page at a time on the main thread, interleaved with rendering.

Were you thinking about parallelizing segmentation too, or was this just a confirmation?

## User

Also, is it possible to align Segment page time output and thread-N page output to the colon char?

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

The user wants to align the per-page output to the colon character. Currently:

```
  Segment page   1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
  [thread-1] page   1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
```

They want the `:` to be at the same column. The issue is that `Segment page   1:` has a different length than `[thread-1] page   1:`.

Let me figure out the lengths:
- `  Segment page   1:` = 2+12+1+3+1 = 19 chars (the `   1` is 4 chars with right-aligned number)
- `  [thread-1] page   1:` = 2+10+1+3+1 = 17 chars (the `   1` is 4 chars with right-aligned number)

Wait, let me count more carefully.
- `  Segment page ` = 2 + 14 = 16 chars
- `  [thread-1] page ` = 2 + 15 = 17 chars

Hmm wait:
- `  Segment page ` = 2 spaces + "Segment page " (13 chars) = 15 chars
- Then the right-aligned number `  1` = 4 chars (3 spaces + 1 digit)
- Then `: ` = 2 chars

Total before the time: 15 + 4 + 2 = 21 chars for "Segment page   1: "

For `[thread-1]`:
- `  [thread-1] page ` = 2 + 15 = 17 chars
- `  1` = 4 chars
- `: ` = 2 chars
Total: 17 + 4 + 2 = 23 chars

Wait that's wrong. Let me be more precise:

"  Segment page " = 2 spaces + "Segment page " (13 chars) = 15 chars
"  1" = 3 spaces + "1" = 4 chars (since it's `%3d`, format with width 3)
": " = 2 chars

So "  Segment page   1: " = 15 + 4 + 2 = 21 chars

"  [thread-1] page " = 2 spaces + "[thread-1] page " = 2 + 15 = 17 chars
"  1" = 4 chars
": " = 2 chars

"  [thread-1] page   1: " = 17 + 4 + 2 = 23 chars

So the segment line has 21 chars before the time, and the thread line has 23 chars. They differ by 2 chars.

To align the colon, I need to pad one of them. For example, add 2 spaces after "Segment page " to make it 15+2 = 17 + 4 + 2 = 23, matching the thread line.

Actually, the simplest approach: make both use the same format. I can add padding to the "Segment page" line to match the width of "[thread-N] page". But thread-N has variable width (thread-1, thread-10, thread-100). However, with an AtomicInteger starting at 1, and worker threads being typically 2-8, the names will be thread-1 through thread-8. So the max length is "thread-100" = 9 chars for the name + brackets = 11.

Actually, since threadCounter starts at 1 and the pool is bounded by workerThreads, the longest name is `thread-` + max(workerThreads, 1). For a typical 2-thread pool, it's `thread-2` (8 chars). For 16 threads, it's `thread-16` (9 chars). For 100 threads (unlikely), it's `thread-100` (10 chars).

The segment line doesn't have a variable-width component, so I can pad it to match the max possible thread name width.

But this is getting complicated. A simpler approach: make the segment line use a fixed-width label that matches the thread line's label width.

Let me think about this differently. The two lines are:

```
  Segment page   1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
  [thread-1] page   1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
```

For the segment line, the format is: `  Segment page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]`
For the thread line, the format is: `  [%s] page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]`

The thread name `%s` has variable length. To align, I could:
1. Pad the segment line's prefix to match the widest thread prefix
2. Or use the same prefix structure

Option 1 is simpler. Let me calculate:
- Thread prefix = `  [` + threadName + `] page ` 
- Max thread name = `thread-` + digits of workerThreads count
- For up to 99 threads: `thread-99` = 9 chars
- Max prefix length = 2 + 9 + 1 + 6 = 18 chars (2 spaces + [thread-99] + " page ")

Wait: `  [thread-99] page ` = 2 + 11 + 6 = 19 chars? No:
- `  ` = 2
- `[thread-99]` = 11
- ` page ` = 6  
Total = 2 + 11 + 6 = 19 chars

For the segment line: `  Segment page ` = 2 + 13 = 15 chars.

To align, I could add 4 spaces after "page" in the segment line: `  Segment page    ` = 19 chars. Then both prefixes would be 19 chars.

But this is fragile. A cleaner approach: format both with the same total padding before the colon.

Actually, the simplest Java approach: instead of worrying about variable thread name widths, I can print the colon at a fixed position using format width specifiers.

Let me think about how to do this with printf:

```java
String segPrefix = "Segment";
String threadPrefix = "[" + tn + "]";

System.out.printf("  %-12s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    segPrefix, i + 1, segTime, segCumulative);
System.out.printf("  %-12s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    threadPrefix, pageIdx + 1, elapsed, cumulative);
```

Where `%-12s` left-pads to 12 chars. Let me calculate:
- "Segment" = 7 chars → padded to 12 with 5 trailing spaces
- "[thread-1]" = 10 chars → padded to 12 with 2 trailing spaces
- "[thread-99]" = 11 chars → padded to 12 with 1 trailing space

Then the format is: `  %-12s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]`

The total prefix before colon: 2 + 12 + 1 + 3 + 1 = 19 chars for both ("  " + pad12 + " page " + num + ":")

But wait, for thread names longer than 12 chars (`thread-999`), it would overflow. But with a bound on thread count, it's fine. Let me use a generous padding like 14 to allow for `thread-9999` (11 chars):

Actually, let me think about what looks good:

```
  Segment       page   1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
  [thread-1]    page   1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
```

Hmm, the `%-Xs` approach puts the padding AFTER the label, which might look a bit odd. Let me try:

```
  Segment       page   1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
  [thread-1]    page   1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
```

Where the label field is left-padded to, say, 13 chars:
- "Segment" + 6 spaces = 13
- "[thread-1]" + 3 spaces = 13
- "[thread-99]" + 2 spaces = 13

Hmm, but for the thread line, the `%s` is the thread name. The prefix would be:
- `  %-13s page %3d: ` = 2 + 13 + 1 + 6 (space+"page"+space) + 3 (right-aligned page num) + 2 (colon+space) = 27 chars before the time

Wait, let me just write the format strings properly:

```java
// Segment line
System.out.printf("  %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    "Segment", i + 1, segTime, segCumulative);

// OCR line  
System.out.printf("  %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    "[" + tn + "]", pageIdx + 1, elapsed, cumulative);
```

This gives:
```
  Segment       page   1: 4.1s [+4.1s to walltime]
  [thread-1]    page   1: 12.2s [+16.3s to walltime]
```

That's pretty clean. The `:` and the time values are perfectly aligned.

But wait, "Segment" is 7 chars, so with %-13s it gets 6 spaces of padding. "[thread-1]" is 10 chars, so it gets 3 spaces. "[thread-99]" is 11 chars, so it gets 2 spaces. And each line starts with 2 spaces.

Let me think about the max thread name. The AtomicInteger starts at 1. For workerThreads = N, the thread names are thread-1, thread-2, ..., thread-N. The max length is "thread-" + digits(N). For up to 999 worker threads, "thread-999" = 10 chars + 2 brackets = 12. So 13 should be safe for any reasonable configuration.

Actually, if we allow `--threads 16`, the max name is "thread-16" = 9 chars + 2 brackets = 11. 13 is safe.

Let me go with `%-13s`.

Wait, I should also consider: do I keep the existing 2-space indent + add the extra 2 spaces (for the user's "add indent" request)? The user said "add indent to per-page output". Currently it's:

```
  Segment page   1: ...
  [thread-1] page   1: ...
```

With 2 leading spaces. The user wants more indent. Let me change from 2 to 4 leading spaces.

So the full format:
```java
System.out.printf("    %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    "Segment", i + 1, segTime, segCumulative);
System.out.printf("    %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n", 
    "[" + tn + "]", pageIdx + 1, elapsed, cumulative);
```

And for the "Segmentation done" and "OCR done" lines, just add 2 extra spaces (4 total):
```
    Segmentation done in 52.9s (10 pages)
    OCR done: 10 pages in 53.0s (2 threads)
```

Let me now implement all these changes.

Total changes:
1. Line 143: `"  Workers:"` → `"  OCR Workers:"`
2. Line 218: `"  Segment page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n"` → `"    %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n"` with `"Segment"` as arg
3. Line 234: `"  [%s] page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n"` → `"    %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n"` with `"[" + tn + "]"` as arg
4. Line 241: `"  Segmentation done"` → `"    Segmentation done"`
5. Line 260: `"  OCR done"` → `"    OCR done"`

## User

Do we really need to show the warning in console output:

Jun 29, 2026 7:34:40 P.M. org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.PDDocument save
WARNING: You are overwriting the existing file output.pdf, this will produce a corrupted file if you're also reading from it 

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

The user is asking about the PDFBox warning about overwriting the existing file. This warning appears because `output.save(outputFile)` is saving to the same file that might already exist from a previous run, and PDFBox warns that if you're also reading from the same file, it could get corrupted. 

Since we write the output file fresh each run (not reading from it), this warning is harmless but annoying. We can suppress it by either:
1. Writing to a temp file and then moving it
2. Suppressing the PDFBox logger for this specific class
3. Deleting the output file before saving

The simplest approach: delete the output file before saving, or save to a temporary file then rename.

Let me check the current code around the save call.

</details>

### Tool: read

**Input:**

"[thread-1]" is 10 chars padded to 13 = 3 extra spaces.

The user wants the colon aligned but without excessive whitespace between the label and "page".

A better approach: instead of padding the label to a fixed width, I should pad the label to fit the MAXIMUM possible label width. Then the "page" and colon parts align naturally.

What's the maximum label length? Thread names are `thread-1` to `thread-N` where N is at most the worker thread count. With `--threads 2`, it's `thread-2` (8 chars) + brackets = 10 chars total. With `--threads 16`, it's `thread-16` (9 chars) + brackets = 11 chars. With `--threads 100`, it's `thread-100` (10 chars) + brackets = 12 chars.

But we don't know the max thread name length at compile time. We could compute it dynamically.

Alternative: just make both labels sit right before "page " with no extra padding, and align the colon after the page number instead. Like:

```
    Segment page   1: 5.0s [+6.2s to walltime]
```

Wait, that's what it was before! The user's complaint is specifically about the alignment to colon. Let me re-read.

The user said "too many white spaces" - referring to the extra spaces between "Segment" and "page" when using `%-13s`.

The format is:
```
    %-13s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]
```

"Segment" (7 chars) → padded to 13 → "Segment       " (7 + 6 spaces)
"[thread-1]" (10 chars) → padded to 13 → "[thread-1]    " (10 + 3 spaces)

So "Segment       page   1:" has 6 spaces between Segment and page.
And "[thread-1]    page   1:" has 3 spaces between ] and page.

That's a lot of whitespace. The user wants alignment with less whitespace.

One approach: use a shorter field width. But then longer thread names won't fit.

Better approach: compute the max thread name length dynamically, then pad to that length.

```java
int maxLabelWidth = Math.max("Segment".length(), maxThreadNameLength);
String segLabel = String.format("%-" + maxLabelWidth + "s", "Segment");
// and similarly for thread label
```

But max thread name length comes from the AtomicInteger's max value which equals workerThreads. So `maxLabelWidth = Math.max("Segment".length(), ("thread-" + workerThreads).length() + 2)`.

For workerThreads = 2:
- "thread-2" = 8 chars + 2 brackets = 10 chars
- maxLabelWidth = max(7, 10) = 10

For workerThreads = 16:
- "thread-16" = 9 chars + 2 brackets = 11 chars
- maxLabelWidth = max(7, 11) = 11

Then the format would be `%-10s page %3d: ...` for 2 threads.

Output:
```
    Segment    page   1: 5.0s [+6.2s to walltime]
    [thread-1] page   1: 14.0s [+20.2s to walltime]
```

"Segment    " = 7 + 3 spaces = 10 chars
"[thread-1] " = 10 + 0 spaces = 10 chars

That's much tighter. Only 3 spaces between "Segment" and "page" instead of 6.

For 16 threads: max width = 11
```
    Segment     page   1: 5.0s [...]
    [thread-16] page   1: 14.0s [...]
```

"Segment     " = 7 + 4 spaces = 11 chars

Still tight.

Let me think about labelWidth calculation:
- `workerThreads` is the pool size
- The thread counter starts at 1, ends at workerThreads
- Max thread name = "thread-" + workerThreads
- But `workerThreads` is int, so "thread-999" etc.
- For practical purposes, `workerThreads <= Runtime.availableProcessors()` which is typically <= 128
- So max label width = "thread-128".length() + 2 = 10 + 2 = 12
- Compare with "Segment".length() = 7
- labelWidth = max(7, 12) = 12 for a 128-thread system (unlikely)
- labelWidth = max(7, 10) = 10 for a 16-thread system

I could also just always use labelWidth = 12 or even 11 to be safe, but dynamic is better.

Actually, I think a clean solution is to compute it based on the thread count:

```java
int maxThreadNameLen = ("thread-" + workerThreads).length() + 2; // e.g., "thread-16" + brackets
int labelWidth = Math.max("Segment".length(), maxThreadNameLen);
final String perPageFormat = "    %-" + labelWidth + "s page %3d: %.1fs [+%.1fs to walltime]%n";
```

Then use `perPageFormat` in both printfs. Since `labelWidth` is final and `perPageFormat` is final, the lambda can capture `perPageFormat`.

This solves the "too many whitespaces" problem by dynamically computing the minimum padding needed.

```java
    ("thread-" + workerThreads).length() + 2  // brackets
```
